    - generic [ref=e1014]: TWOW1
    - heading "Two-Way Door Architecture" [level=2] [ref=e1016]:
      - link "Two-Way Door Architecture" [ref=e1017] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#TWOW1"
    - generic [ref=e1018]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e1019]: 4 Jan 2026
    - generic [ref=e1020]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e1021]: vibe-coding, software-design, architecture, ai
    - generic [ref=e1022]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e1023]: Amazon tells us to avoid one-way doors. This is critical with vibe coding. There are architectural and software design decisions that are one-way doors, and vibe coding is incredibly dangerous when built with these patterns. The challenge for human developers is to detect the one-way doors and vibe code without them. Using automated verification, coding agents can run very fast and go back through the two-way doors when needed. Guardrails can also be built with these patterns, allowing the whole pipeline to run at top speed.

    - generic [ref=e1027]: BEYO1
    - heading "Beyond the Ritual" [level=2] [ref=e1029]:
      - link "Beyond the Ritual" [ref=e1030] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#BEYO1"
    - generic [ref=e1031]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e1032]: 4 Jan 2026
    - generic [ref=e1033]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e1034]: camus, ritual, absurdism, philosophy
    - generic [ref=e1035]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e1036]: Rituals connect us to each other. Rituals are a shorthand which allows us to quickly see where other people fit in. However, rituals can become unmoored and mismatched from reality. They become absurd when Camus's Meursault doesn't perform the rituals expected—for example, crying at his mother's funeral or responding affirmatively to Marie's marriage proposal—he becomes disconnected. He's convicted because of this disconnect. When he confronts the chaplain, he realizes the absurdity of human action and life and finally understands that he is happy through his real connection beyond the ritual.

She knows I love going to the movies, especially alone.
    - paragraph [ref=e1050]: Today is the first day I am free to see something. I selected _The Stranger_, adapted from Camus' novella. I hadn't read it before, and I wanted to familiarise myself with the plot and philosophical themes. So this morning I listened to the entire audiobook.

  - article [ref=e1320]:
    - generic [ref=e1321]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e1322]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e1323]: JACK1
    - heading "Jackson Embraces Physicalism" [level=2] [ref=e1325]:
      - link "Jackson Embraces Physicalism" [ref=e1326] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#JACK1"
    - generic [ref=e1327]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e1328]: 30 Jun 2025
    - generic [ref=e1329]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e1330]: philosophy, consciousness, qualia, physicalism, epistemology, intuition
    - generic [ref=e1331]: illus
    - generic [ref=e1332]:
      - img "a digital artwork showcases a striking bauhaus-inspired design, dominated by bold red, blue, yellow, and black, set against a textured beige backdrop. a large red circle sits off-center, intersecting with blue and yellow rectangles, while thick black lines divide the composition into dynamic sections"
    - generic [ref=e1333]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e1334]: Frank Jackson, the champion of the knowledge argument, recanted.
    - paragraph [ref=e1335]: He decided Mary's new experience, seeing red for the first time, could be explained without depending on non-physical facts. To Jackson, what Mary gained was not new propositional information, but a new perspective on physical facts she already knew.
    - paragraph [ref=e1336]: Reflecting on the weakness of his argument, Jackson noted that Mary "learning something new" felt intuitive but was semantically misleading. The words "knows" and "learns" hid an ambiguity, confusing propositional knowledge with phenomenal acquaintance. Upon distinguishing these, the knowledge argument loses its force.
    - paragraph [ref=e1337]: Ultimately, Jackson discarded his influential contribution and accepted physicalism.
    - generic [ref=e1338]: links
    - list [ref=e1340]:
      - listitem [ref=e1341]:

          - /url: "#MARY1"

          - /url: "#ABIL1"

        - text: "-"
        - link "\"Mind and Illusion\"" [ref=e1346] [cursor=pointer]:
          - /url: https://www.informationphilosopher.com/solutions/philosophers/jackson/Mind_And_Illusion.pdf
        - text: by Frank Jackson, Royal Institute of Philosophy Supplement 53 (2003) ("Although I once dissented from the majority, I have capitulated and now see the interesting issue as being where the arguments from the intuitions against physicalism—-the arguments that seem so compelling—-go wrong.")
      - listitem [ref=e1347]:
        - text: "-"
        - link "_There's Something About Mary_" [ref=e1348] [cursor=pointer]:

          - emphasis [ref=e1349]: _There's Something About Mary_
        - text: by Peter Ludlow, Yujin Nagasawa & Daniel Stoljar (eds.)

          - emphasis [ref=e1352]: _Phenomenal Concepts and Phenomenal Knowledge_

  - article [ref=e1353]:
    - generic [ref=e1354]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e1355]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e1356]: ABIL1
    - heading "Ability, Not Fact" [level=2] [ref=e1358]:
      - link "Ability, Not Fact" [ref=e1359] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#ABIL1"
    - generic [ref=e1360]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e1361]: 21 Jun 2025
    - generic [ref=e1362]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e1363]: philosophy, consciousness, qualia, physicalism, knowledge, ability
    - generic [ref=e1364]: illus
    - generic [ref=e1365]:
      - 'img "a flat, mid-century-style graphic: three stacked icons (eye, person silhouette, open hand) on the left with an orange arrow pointing right toward a black profile head; inside the head a stylized blue brain holds a red circle, symbolizing a new neural ability"'
    - generic [ref=e1366]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e1367]: When Mary first sees red, the experience leaves a neural impression, but she does not learn a non-physical fact. She gains an ability to recognise red, mirroring an ability others already possessed; she did not learn something about those people. Calling this "know-how" knowledge overextends the word "know". This echoes the "ability hypothesis" counter-argument by David Lewis and Laurence Nemirow. Because Mary gains a new ability through this experience that is reducible to physical explanation, no new non-physical fact like qualia is required. While intuitive, Jackson's "knowledge argument" about Mary's room fails to achieve its goal of disproving physicalism.
    - generic [ref=e1368]: links

        - text: by David Lewis

  - article [ref=e1379]:
    - generic [ref=e1380]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e1381]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e1382]: MARY1
    - heading "Mary Sees Red" [level=2] [ref=e1384]:
      - link "Mary Sees Red" [ref=e1385] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#MARY1"
    - generic [ref=e1386]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e1387]: 19 Jun 2025
    - generic [ref=e1388]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e1389]: philosophy, colour, experience, consciousness, qualia, physicalism
    - generic [ref=e1390]: illus
    - generic [ref=e1391]:
      - img "abstract color-field painting with a soft gradient gray area on the left blending into an intense solid red block on the right, the two hues meeting in a hazy vertical transition"
    - generic [ref=e1392]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e1393]: Mary grew up in a black-and-white room and learned every fact about colour and about people who see colours, without ever seeing something colourful herself. One day she left the room and saw a red object for the first time. For Frank Jackson, Mary learned something about others upon seeing red that she couldn't have learned in the room. She gained some new factual knowledge, a knowledge of what others experience when they see red. This could not have been gained inside the room. Because Mary's physical knowledge from inside the room was incomplete, Jackson concluded physicalism was false.

    - generic [ref=e1480]: SELF1
    - heading "Self-Serving Misunderstanding" [level=2] [ref=e1482]:
      - link "Self-Serving Misunderstanding" [ref=e1483] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#SELF1"
    - generic [ref=e1484]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e1485]: 22 May 2025
    - generic [ref=e1486]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e1487]: self, misunderstanding, communication, language, mind, perception
    - generic [ref=e1488]: illus
    - generic [ref=e1489]:
      - img "two stylized figures in a sci-fi setting, one in orange deep in thought and the other in blue gesturing toward them, with a starry sky and distant planet in the background, rendered in moebius-style ink and color without any speech or thought bubbles"
    - generic [ref=e1490]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e1491]: Accurate communication is often difficult. A lot is usually lost in the translation from idea to utterance to percept to idea. From the set of possibly intended meanings for a perceived message, I sometimes don't select the most plausible. My conscious and unconscious desires bias me towards interpretations which suit me better, interpretations I wish had been meant.
    - paragraph [ref=e1492]: When I seek clarity and confirmation, I can curtail this bias, narrowing the set. I make substantially implausible the meanings that I just want to hear. Reflection, in the moment and later, can also challenge me against succumbing to lazy, selfish interpretations.

    - generic [ref=e1500]: pubAt

    - generic [ref=e1504]: illus
    - generic [ref=e1505]:
      - img "a man in a dark suit stands facing an empty picture frame on a museum wall, with fluffy white clouds floating both inside and outside the frame against a soft blue and beige background in a surreal, flat design style"
    - generic [ref=e1506]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e1507]: A rifle leans against the wall, coated with blood.
    - paragraph [ref=e1508]: The scene is still, the rifle balanced, the blood settled. All is calm. Painting as a medium is necessarily static. But the image tells of action. Violence. The image is cold, but tells us about hot emotion.
    - paragraph [ref=e1509]: The blood on the gun is strange. The barrel of the gun is oddly spotless. The stain starts from the middle and continues back. Only towards the shooter, instead of the victim. There are lines of blood down the stock, almost as if the rifle itself bled out after being placed against the wall.

    - generic [ref=e1653]: VIBE1
    - heading "Vibe Coding Tools" [level=2] [ref=e1655]:
      - link "Vibe Coding Tools" [ref=e1656] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#VIBE1"
    - generic [ref=e1657]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e1658]: 7 Apr 2025
    - generic [ref=e1659]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e1660]: ai, vibe-coding, tools
    - generic [ref=e1661]: illus
    - generic [ref=e1662]:
      - img "a digital illustration in a modern, geometric, and futuristic style showing a conductor orchestrating a glowing ai-powered pipeline with symbols and icons, against a dark background with blue and purple gradients"
    - generic [ref=e1663]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e1664]: I've been exploring vibe coding patterns. I have built my 'dadeto' blog generator without any lines coded by hand. I have a few different tools for different phases. I use 4o or o3-mini in ChatGPT to build a one-shot prototype and its first test suite. I then insert it into new files in Windsurf and have Claude 3.7 or Gemini 2.5 integrate it into my project. I then use Stryker and Jest to tell 4o what tests to add to get logical coverage to 100%. Finally, I refactor, using 4o for single-file changes and Windsurf for multi-file changes.

    - generic [ref=e1728]: FLUS1
    - heading "Flushing Out Ideas" [level=2] [ref=e1730]:
      - link "Flushing Out Ideas" [ref=e1731] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#FLUS1"
    - generic [ref=e1732]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e1733]: 4 Apr 2025
    - generic [ref=e1734]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e1735]: writing, quality, ideas
    - generic [ref=e1736]: illus
    - generic [ref=e1737]:
      - img "a person sitting at a desk writing on paper, surrounded by crumpled pages and a glowing lightbulb above their head, symbolizing the creative process of refining ideas"
    - generic [ref=e1738]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e1739]: Since 2012, my blog posts have followed a constrained writing format. Exactly three word titles. Exactly one-hundred word bodies. The idea was to reduce the friction to writing blog posts. I remember hearing once that all your good ideas are stuck in a queue behind a thousand bad ideas. To get to the good ideas, you must flush out the bad ideas first. You have to iterate and not get attached. If you are prolific enough, you'll get them out and then you'll finally start getting good ideas coming out. My blog generator is now getting rid of more friction.

    - heading "_Getting Things Done_" [level=2] [ref=e2021]:
      - link "_Getting Things Done_" [ref=e2022] [cursor=pointer]:

    - generic [ref=e2023]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2024]: 15 Mar 2025
    - generic [ref=e2025]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2026]: productivity, organisation, mental-health
    - generic [ref=e2027]: illus
    - img "a japanese zen-inspired ink drawing of a minimalist desk beneath a vast, open sky. the desk contains a blank-paged journal, a pen, and a small, simple inbox holding a few odd items. the expressive ink strokes create a sense of balance, calmness, and clarity, resembling traditional sumi-e or shodō ink art. the composition conveys mental organization and peace." [ref=e2029]
    - generic [ref=e2030]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2031]: During the COVID-19 pandemic, I felt like I was in a fog.
    - paragraph [ref=e2032]: I desperately downloaded _Getting Things Done_ as an audiobook. I adopted its recommendations as I listened. In the last five years, I have been practicing and much is now automatic.
    - paragraph [ref=e2033]: "The most valuable insight for me was one idea:"
    - paragraph [ref=e2034]: Keeping things in my head caused much of my stress.
    - paragraph [ref=e2035]: The stress shrunk upon writing things down. Of course, without organisation, you won't trust your notes and your stress will creep back.
    - paragraph [ref=e2036]: But back then, I needed to start small.
    - paragraph [ref=e2037]: Start with writing things down.

    - generic [ref=e2050]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e2051]: SYMP1
    - heading "Sympathy for P-Zombies" [level=2] [ref=e2053]:
      - link "Sympathy for P-Zombies" [ref=e2054] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#SYMP1"
    - generic [ref=e2055]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2056]: 10 Mar 2025
    - generic [ref=e2057]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2058]: consciousness, philosophy, sympathy
    - generic [ref=e2059]: illus
    - img "a cyberpunk-style illustration of a person standing before a mirror in a neon-lit futuristic cityscape. the reflection in the mirror is not their own but a smooth, featureless mannequin, creating an eerie contrast. the person’s edges subtly glitch, hinting at digital distortion. cool blue and purple neon lights cast an otherworldly glow, emphasizing the unsettling atmosphere. the background features a blurred cyberpunk city, reinforcing the theme of technology and identity" [ref=e2061]
    - generic [ref=e2062]: audio
    - generic [ref=e2063]:
      - link "PLAY" [ref=e2064] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#"
      - link "PAUSE" [ref=e2065] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#"
      - link "STOP" [ref=e2066] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#"
      - text: 0:00
    - generic [ref=e2067]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2068]: This might sound like psychopathy, but bear with me.
    - paragraph [ref=e2069]: There is an important difference between _empathy_ and _sympathy_. _Empathy_ is sharing some of the same conscious experiences as another person. _Sympathy_ is instead feeling compassion towards someone else who is suffering. Empathy requires a relation between you and a conscious experience. Sympathy does not.
    - paragraph [ref=e2070]: A philosophical zombie appears to have conscious experience but lacks it. A P-zombie can still suffer but it lacks conscious experiences. So, one can sympathise with a P-zombie but cannot empathise with it.
    - paragraph [ref=e2071]: If I doubt meaningful conscious experiences exist, then I must doubt empathy exists.
    - generic [ref=e2072]: links
    - list [ref=e2074]:
      - listitem [ref=e2075]:

        - text: by @AdjectiveAlli

  - article [ref=e2087]:
    - generic [ref=e2088]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e2089]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e2090]: DETE1
    - heading "Deterministic Moral Preferences" [level=2] [ref=e2092]:
      - link "Deterministic Moral Preferences" [ref=e2093] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#DETE1"
    - generic [ref=e2094]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2095]: 8 Oct 2024
    - generic [ref=e2096]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2097]: determinism, morality, politics
    - generic [ref=e2098]: illus
    - img "A pointillist painting of an Italian town square with muted colors, showing a crowd of people discussing something important." [ref=e2100]
    - generic [ref=e2101]: audio
    - generic [ref=e2102]:
      - link "PLAY" [ref=e2103] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#"
      - link "PAUSE" [ref=e2104] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#"
      - link "STOP" [ref=e2105] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#"
      - text: 0:00
    - generic [ref=e2106]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2107]: As a deterministic agent, you still have preferences. (These are pre-defined and moulded by external factors.) You prefer "good" outcomes, "good" intentions, and "right" actions. Your preferences combine and collide with those of others in your communities. These form social expectations. Community members formalise these and enforce them with rewards and punishments. You do not receive rewards and punishments due to your "moral responsibility". You receive them due to your (mis)alignment with the community. But this alignment goes both ways. Moral progress is a judgement of increasing alignment with your current preferences. Current misalignment encourages you to be political.

  - article [ref=e2142]:
    - generic [ref=e2143]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e2144]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e2145]: ADJE1
    - heading "Adjectives and Properties" [level=2] [ref=e2147]:
      - link "Adjectives and Properties" [ref=e2148] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#ADJE1"
    - generic [ref=e2149]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2150]: 26 Jul 2024
    - generic [ref=e2151]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2152]: philosophy, language, properties
    - generic [ref=e2153]: illus
    - img "An illustration of a child deep in thought, surrounded by thought bubbles. One bubble shows an ant labeled ‘big’, another shows the Milky Way. Nearby are skeletons, pensive old men, and more ants." [ref=e2155]
    - generic [ref=e2156]: audio
    - generic [ref=e2157]:
      - link "PLAY" [ref=e2158] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#"
      - link "PAUSE" [ref=e2159] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#"
      - link "STOP" [ref=e2160] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#"
      - text: 0:00
    - generic [ref=e2161]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2162]: I was eight years old when I had my earliest metaphysical reflection. (This excludes religious doubt.)
    - paragraph [ref=e2163]: "My thoughts:"
    - paragraph [ref=e2164]: It was obvious that “bigger” and “smaller” were adjectives describing relations between objects. But “big” and “small” were also relations between objects and not properties of a single object. I am big compared to an ant, and small compared to the Milky Way, but I am neither big nor small alone. I conjected that there were no properties, only relations. I am not “alive” alone; I am more alive than someone in a vegetative state, who is more alive than a skeleton.
  - article [ref=e2165]:
    - generic [ref=e2166]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄

    - paragraph [ref=e2185]: _Lonely and Horny_ was a web video series created and acted by Jake Hurwitz and Amir Blumenfeld. Amir plays an obnoxious character named Ruby who only cares about himself. There’s an interesting speech made by Ruby in _Lonely and Horny_. He gives a spirited, funny, and coherent defence of philosophical egoism that I haven’t seen often.
    - blockquote [ref=e2186]:
      - paragraph [ref=e2199]: _"I'm the only one that I am, dude… I don't care about *other* people, because they're not me. … Me is the only feelings I know and can have, so I’m invested in *my* feelings *being good* for *me*."_

    - generic [ref=e2207]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄

    - generic [ref=e2209]: ETHI1
    - heading "Ethics' Divine Suspension" [level=2] [ref=e2211]:
      - link "Ethics' Divine Suspension" [ref=e2212] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#ETHI1"
    - generic [ref=e2213]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2214]: 6 Apr 2024
    - generic [ref=e2215]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2216]: philosophy, ethics, religion
    - generic [ref=e2217]: illus
    - img "An old Bible-style illustration of Abraham and Isaac at the moment of divine intervention." [ref=e2219]
    - generic [ref=e2220]: audio
    - generic [ref=e2221]:
      - link "PLAY" [ref=e2222] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#"
      - link "PAUSE" [ref=e2223] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#"
      - link "STOP" [ref=e2224] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#"
      - text: 0:00
    - generic [ref=e2225]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2226]: Søren Kierkegaard wrote in "Fear and Trembling" about Abraham sacrificing his son. Despite its contradiction with universal moral norms, Abraham was willing to obey God. This is Kierkegaard's example of "teleological suspension of the ethical". Here, the duty to obey God supersedes moral duty. I'm interested in coherent moral codes, which allow for determinate decision-making. I would frame this obedience to God as a "trumping" duty within a pluralistic moral framework. Reasoning drives quotidian decisions but a command from God would override that all. Kierkegaard appears to object to fitting this sublime duty of obedience into morality. Seems odd.
  - article [ref=e2227]:
    - generic [ref=e2228]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e2229]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e2230]: HOLD1
    - heading "Holding Strong Opinions" [level=2] [ref=e2232]:
      - link "Holding Strong Opinions" [ref=e2233] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#HOLD1"
    - generic [ref=e2234]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2235]: 2 Apr 2024
    - generic [ref=e2236]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2237]: philosophy, ethics, moral-pluralism
    - generic [ref=e2238]: illus
    - generic [ref=e2239]:
      - img "An abstract representation of decision-making complexity, featuring a maze illuminated by a guiding light, surrounded by symbols of wisdom such as an open book, a compass, and interconnected nodes."
    - generic [ref=e2240]: audio
    - generic [ref=e2241]:
      - link "PLAY" [ref=e2242] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#"
      - link "PAUSE" [ref=e2243] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#"
      - link "STOP" [ref=e2244] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#"
      - text: 0:00
    - generic [ref=e2245]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2246]: "\"Strong opinions, held lightly.\" Indecision is paralysing. Knowledge is unreachable. Without certainty, how do we decide?"
    - paragraph [ref=e2247]: "Effective decision-making requires:"
    - paragraph [ref=e2248]: "1. Pragmatism: favour action over perfection."
    - paragraph [ref=e2249]: "2. Probabilistic reasoning: balance possible outcomes."
    - paragraph [ref=e2250]: "3. Fallibilism: accept that you will often be wrong."
    - paragraph [ref=e2251]: "4. Ethical consideration: link your options to your moral values."
    - paragraph [ref=e2252]: "5. Community wisdom: seek feedback and collective experience."
    - paragraph [ref=e2253]: By factoring these into each choice we make, we build habits and intuitions. As we reflect and practice, we gain speed and accuracy. Poor decisions also guide us to invest more in learning and discovery. In the end, we gain humility, empathy, and confidence to move forward.
  - article [ref=e2254]:
    - generic [ref=e2255]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e2256]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e2257]: APPR1
    - heading "Appreciating Moral Pluralism" [level=2] [ref=e2259]:
      - link "Appreciating Moral Pluralism" [ref=e2260] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#APPR1"
    - generic [ref=e2261]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2262]: 10 Sept 2023
    - generic [ref=e2263]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2264]: philosophy, pluralism, dilemmas
    - generic [ref=e2265]: illus
    - generic [ref=e2266]:
      - img "Artistic representation of the Trolley Problem in a surreal environment. The tracks twist and turn in impossible ways, emphasizing the complexity of the decision. On one track, five figures stand with linked arms, while a lone figure stands defiantly on the other. Above, a constellation in the shape of scales balances the ethical concepts of harm caused and harm allowed."
    - generic [ref=e2267]: audio
    - generic [ref=e2268]:
      - link "PLAY" [ref=e2269] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#"
      - link "PAUSE" [ref=e2270] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#"
      - link "STOP" [ref=e2271] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#"
      - text: 0:00
    - generic [ref=e2272]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2273]: In the Trolley Problem, a runaway trolley is about to kill five people. You can divert it to another track, but if you do, another person will die instead. Many disagree about whether to divert the trolley. Some would choose to divert the trolley to minimize the number harmed. Others would refuse to divert the trolley, possibly because harm caused is worse than harm allowed. The lack of consensus can teach us about moral pluralism. Moral pluralism means different people have different moral values and moral codes. The Trolley Problem offers a framework for discussing and appreciating actual moral pluralism.
  - article [ref=e2274]:
    - generic [ref=e2275]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e2276]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e2277]: COMP1
    - heading "Competing Environmental Causes" [level=2] [ref=e2279]:
      - link "Competing Environmental Causes" [ref=e2280] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#COMP1"
    - generic [ref=e2281]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2282]: 20 Aug 2023
    - generic [ref=e2283]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2284]: environment, trade-offs, extraction
    - generic [ref=e2285]: illus
    - img "Oil painting illustrating the central role of battery technology in combating climate change. A large battery connects a polluted, smog-filled world on one side to a cleaner, greener world on the other. Miners labor in tough conditions while a parched landscape depicts the water-intensive nature of lithium extraction. Adjacent to this, a technologically advanced battery production facility stands tall, symbolizing hope." [ref=e2287]
    - generic [ref=e2288]: audio
    - generic [ref=e2289]:
      - link "PLAY" [ref=e2290] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#"
      - link "PAUSE" [ref=e2291] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#"
      - link "STOP" [ref=e2292] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#"
      - text: 0:00
    - generic [ref=e2293]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2294]: Battery technology is crucial for mitigating climate change. But the materials often come from countries with lax environmental and labor protections. For example, there are reports of child labour and forced labour in the extraction of cobalt in the DRC. Lithium extraction is also water-intensive, impacting local ecosystems. Pausing or delaying the mining isn't the solution. Mining is essential for the transition away from fossil fuels. Instead, the focus should be on improving conditions and ethical sourcing. Failure to raise environmental and labour standards will extend our fossil fuel dependency. This will only entrench the harm of fossil fuel mining.

    - generic [ref=e2338]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2339]: AI is taking off, and this year brought new developments like GPT-4, Stable Diffusion XL, and llama.cpp. Even if all progress paused, the tools we already have could reshape society. Guessing what ten more years will look like is hard. We already struggle to predict the current pace of technology. We also can't be sure how people or governments will respond to these new tools. We don't know how they might want to direct its development. We should be careful and thoughtful, taking responsibility for how we use it. All while assuming everyone else will rush to use it.

    - generic [ref=e2396]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2397]: Look closer. The right side of South America once snuggled into the left side of Africa. For hundreds of years, since the development of good global maps, we suspected it but didn't _know_ it.
    - paragraph [ref=e2398]: "A century ago, geologists too were in a process of upheaval. In 1915, Alfred Wegener outlined this theory in _The Origins of Continents and Oceans_. But Wegener had more than a map: he had fossils."
    - paragraph [ref=e2399]: _Mesosaurus_ was too little of a croc to swim across oceans, but lived on both sides.
    - paragraph [ref=e2400]: And _Glossopteris_ was a seed fern whose seeds were too fat to blow so far.

    - generic [ref=e2427]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2428]: I'm tired. It's not so bad. Others have it worse. But I'm tired.
    - paragraph [ref=e2429]: Last year was quite a year, but my close friends and family didn't get COVID-19. My relationships have strengthened despite the stress. Worrying about elderly neighbours and isolating with bored children didn't wear me down.
    - paragraph [ref=e2430]: But it wore me out.
    - paragraph [ref=e2431]: Every day is on autopilot with dozens of easy 5-minute tasks left undone for days. A brain fog smothers me from morning til night.
    - paragraph [ref=e2432]: The thing about walking through a fog is that you don't know where it ends. How much further do we have to go?

    - generic [ref=e2436]: THES1
    - heading "_The Sixth Extinction_" [level=2] [ref=e2438]:
      - link "_The Sixth Extinction_" [ref=e2439] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#THES1"
    - generic [ref=e2440]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2441]: 26 Dec 2019
    - generic [ref=e2442]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2443]: extinction, human-nature, climate
    - generic [ref=e2444]: illus
    - generic [ref=e2445]:
      - img "the Panamanian rainforest, oil painting"
    - generic [ref=e2446]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2447]: Free will has a problem. Either one chooses for a reason or for no reason. Either it's not a choice but an effect or it's not a choice but a dice roll. Either way, it's hard to blame someone like that.
    - paragraph [ref=e2448]: _The Sixth Extinction_ surveys human irruption and decimation of other species. It doesn't lay blame. At a species level, the course of human history seems to be a mix of mere causality and being lucky. There's no call-to-action at the end of the book, because it's not clear there is an action anyone can take to reverse human nature.

    - generic [ref=e2462]: CLIM1
    - heading "Climate Change Optimism" [level=2] [ref=e2464]:
      - link "Climate Change Optimism" [ref=e2465] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#CLIM1"
    - generic [ref=e2466]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2467]: 16 Dec 2019
    - generic [ref=e2468]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2469]: climate, activism, hope
    - generic [ref=e2470]: illus
    - generic [ref=e2471]:
      - img "hundreds of protestors in front of the brandenburger tor, dancing, red pencil sketch"
    - generic [ref=e2472]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2473]: Climate change is likely to cause hundreds of millions of extra people to die this century. Billions more will suffer. It cannot be “stopped”. In the face of this, grief is reasonable.
    - paragraph [ref=e2474]: "But preventing climate change is not binary: not only success or only failure. It is a matter of degree. With every degree increased, millions more suffer. 6° is worse than 5° and 4°."
    - paragraph [ref=e2475]: In turn, 4° is better than 5° and 6°. With every degree averted, millions more avoid suffering. So, even small actions have tremendous impacts. Choosing to avoid meat, or cycle, or catch a train helps.

    - generic [ref=e2486]: COST1
    - heading "Cost of Glue" [level=2] [ref=e2488]:
      - link "Cost of Glue" [ref=e2489] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#COST1"
    - generic [ref=e2490]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2491]: 10 Dec 2019
    - generic [ref=e2492]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2493]: software, complexity, trade-offs
    - generic [ref=e2494]: illus
    - generic [ref=e2495]:
      - img "a tangle of yellow and black woollen yarn"
    - generic [ref=e2496]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2497]: Modularisation in software design is about breaking down simple collections of complicated components into complex collections of simple components. A complicated component acts incorrectly when you cannot understand its internal behavior. A complex collection acts unexpectedly when you cannot understand their external interactions.
    - paragraph [ref=e2498]: All non-trivial software is both complicated and complex, and we can refactor to trade-off between complicated and complex designs. Object-oriented programmers explored these trade-offs for the last few decades and now microservice architects are exploring them too.
    - paragraph [ref=e2499]: Since working with a few microservice designs, I'm starting to see some trade-offs, especially with tracing interactions and serialisation overhead.
    - generic [ref=e2500]: links
    - list [ref=e2502]:

    - generic [ref=e2514]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2515]: 6 May 2019
    - generic [ref=e2516]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2517]: voting, regret, civic-duty
    - generic [ref=e2518]: illus
    - generic [ref=e2519]:
      - img "european flags, flapping gently in the wind, nighttime photograph"
    - generic [ref=e2520]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2521]: I missed the deadline to register to vote in Germany for the European Union elections. I am very proud of having voted in every national and local election I could, until now. I’m not proud of every vote, but proud that I voted.
    - paragraph [ref=e2522]: "I have lots of excuses for missing the registration deadline:"
    - paragraph [ref=e2523]: 1. Brexit
    - paragraph [ref=e2524]: 2. Taking a working holiday in New Zealand
    - paragraph [ref=e2525]: 3. The deadline being on a Sunday
    - paragraph [ref=e2526]: 4. Not having a printer for my form
    - paragraph [ref=e2527]: But they’re all bad excuses.
    - paragraph [ref=e2528]: I knew that I could have been eligible, and should have sorted out my registration well in advance. I’m ashamed now.

    - generic [ref=e2540]: OPPO1
    - heading "Opportunity Cost Considerations" [level=2] [ref=e2542]:
      - link "Opportunity Cost Considerations" [ref=e2543] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#OPPO1"
    - generic [ref=e2544]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2545]: 7 Mar 2017
    - generic [ref=e2546]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2547]: economics, ethics, priorities
    - generic [ref=e2548]: illus
    - generic [ref=e2549]:
      - img "muted colourful overlapping triangles, digital art"
    - generic [ref=e2550]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2551]: Benjamin Franklin said that time was money. You could have earned ten shillings today, but you spent half of it sitting idle. You only got five shillings for the half-day you did work. To Franklin, you spent (or threw away) the other five shillings in front of the television. Suppose we talk about ten thousand dollars instead. You would likely save a child’s life by donating ten thousand dollars worth of mosquito nets. Have you spent (or thrown away) that child’s life by not fighting for a raise? Or upgrading a good car for a newer model?

    - generic [ref=e2570]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2571]: 1. What single thing could you do differently that will have the biggest positive impact?
    - paragraph [ref=e2572]: 2. When was the last time you should have admitted you were wrong, but didn’t?
    - paragraph [ref=e2573]: 3. Did you discover a hobby that you need to make more time for?
    - paragraph [ref=e2574]: 4. Are there any books you wish you had read earlier?
    - paragraph [ref=e2575]: 5. Any books you wish you had never read?
    - paragraph [ref=e2576]: 6. Is there anybody you keep meaning to catch up with?
    - paragraph [ref=e2577]: 7. Do you have any good habits that you are struggling to make stick?
    - paragraph [ref=e2578]: 8. Do you have any bad habits that you are struggling to drop?
    - paragraph [ref=e2579]: 9. Are you happy?

    - heading "Muted Star Trek" [level=2] [ref=e2585]:
      - link "Muted Star Trek" [ref=e2586] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#MUTE1"
    - generic [ref=e2587]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2588]: 7 Oct 2016
    - generic [ref=e2589]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2590]: television, parenting, nostalgia
    - generic [ref=e2591]: illus
    - generic [ref=e2592]:
      - img "a desert planet with rocks in the background and a colourful sky, pencil and watercolour"
    - generic [ref=e2593]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2594]: Our daughter is almost two, and we now also have a two-month old son. As I settled him back to sleep after his night feeds, I watched the original series of Star Trek on mute with subtitles. After a great first season I ordered some wireless headphones and continued watching. When they arrived I excitedly set them up, but the sound was jarring; I felt like somebody had taken Kirk, Spock, and McCoy and dubbed over their original voices. Of course I knew these were their voices, but I couldn’t continue watching without feeling like the show had become corrupted.
    - paragraph [ref=e2595]: "My favourite _Star Trek_ episodes so far:"
    - paragraph [ref=e2596]: 1. _The City on the Edge of Forever_ (season 1, episode 28)
    - paragraph [ref=e2597]: 2. _The Deadly Years_ (season 2, episode 12)
    - paragraph [ref=e2598]: 3. _The Trouble with Tribbles_ (season 2, episode 15)
    - paragraph [ref=e2599]: 4. _A Private Little War_ (season 2, episode 19)

    - generic [ref=e2603]: PERM1
    - heading "Permitted Moral Actions" [level=2] [ref=e2605]:
      - link "Permitted Moral Actions" [ref=e2606] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#PERM1"
    - generic [ref=e2607]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2608]: 13 Feb 2016
    - generic [ref=e2609]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2610]: philosophy, ethics, decision-making
    - generic [ref=e2611]: illus
    - generic [ref=e2612]:
      - img "an infinite loop of small buddha statues made out of gold"
    - generic [ref=e2613]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2614]: "There are three categories of moral actions: forbidden, required, and permitted. Forbidden actions are those which one must not do. Required actions are those which one must do. Permitted actions are those which one may or may not do. When searching for a moral code to guide their lives, people look for a system of principles, justifications, and directions for how to act. This makes restrictive religious standards popular and powerful. Alternatively, if a moral code primarily consists of only permitted actions, people can't use the moral code to make decisive choices because they are given too much wiggle room."

      - link "Provoking Islamophobic Backlash" [ref=e2629] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#PROV1"
    - generic [ref=e2630]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2631]: 16 Nov 2015
    - generic [ref=e2632]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2633]: terrorism, refugees, media
    - generic [ref=e2634]: illus
    - generic [ref=e2635]:
      - img "a nighttime aerial view of inner paris, with the eiffel tower lit up in the background, photograph"
    - generic [ref=e2636]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2637]: If we assume that the Islamic State coordinated the November 2015 attacks on Paris, it should be quickly obvious that such an attack intended to encourage a backlash against Islamic refugees. The cynic in me entertains the possibility that the Syrian passport found by one killer's body was a prop to brew distrust of all Syrian refugees. It is unknown whether it belonged to the killer. All of the other suspects or identified killers appear to be Belgian or French. To retaliate against refugees who are fleeing these killers would compound the tragedy. I hope for a calm, measured response.

    - paragraph [ref=e2684]: The second trailer to _Jurassic World_ shows Beth (Bryce Dallas Howard) telling Owen (Chris Pratt) that the scientists have genetically modified a dinosaur to keep the park audiences interested. I reacted similarly and rolled my eyes when they mentioned genetic modification in the first trailer, but now think that maybe the filmmakers are commenting on the nature of the _Jurassic Park_ film franchise. The filmmakers clearly thought that the new film also needed something extra to keep their audiences interested.

    - heading "_Snow Crash_ Thoughts" [level=2] [ref=e2699]:
      - link "_Snow Crash_ Thoughts" [ref=e2700] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#SNOW1"
    - generic [ref=e2701]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2702]: 31 Mar 2015
    - generic [ref=e2703]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2704]: technology, virtual-reality, social-networks
    - generic [ref=e2705]: illus
    - generic [ref=e2706]:
      - img "cyberpunk vr motorcycles racing to deliver pizza, digital sketches"
    - generic [ref=e2707]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2708]: Immersive virtual reality experiences may be only years, if not months, away. Despite enthusiasts' suspicion and hostility to the announcement, Facebook's acquisition of Oculus gives it a prime position for hosting the Street-like public hub. However, the majority of social interactions will be in private rooms like the Black Sun and Hiro Protagonist's office in _Snow Crash_. A federated social network could allow a heavy user to self-host and a casual user to connect through Facebook and both share the same virtual space. Rooms could establish realism standards and exclude avatars which break the immersion, encouraging investment in new designs.

    - generic [ref=e2722]: THEP1
    - heading "The Post-Apocalyptic Microchip" [level=2] [ref=e2724]:
      - link "The Post-Apocalyptic Microchip" [ref=e2725] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#THEP1"
    - generic [ref=e2726]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2727]: 31 Mar 2015
    - generic [ref=e2728]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2729]: technology, infrastructure, fragility
    - generic [ref=e2730]: illus
    - generic [ref=e2731]:
      - img "Post-Apocalyptic Microchip, mixed media installation"
    - generic [ref=e2732]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2733]: If World War III ended with a few scrappy scavengers in a desolate, nuclear wasteland, I can imagine several technologies being easy to rebuild. A wheel is simple and could be crudely constructed by anyone. With some engineering experience, you could likely find the scraps and basic tools needed to build something like a water pump. But could even the greatest engineer in electronics rebuild or repair a CPU? Blunt tools would be insufficient for the nano-scale precision required. Some materials, such as rare earth metals, might be impossible to find. Some technology production clearly relies on multibillion dollar infrastructure.

    - paragraph [ref=e2755]: Emily St. James claimed that _Gone Girl_ is a feminist film, but her article seems more interested in shocking up page views than in making a convincing argument. While I can see _Gone Girl_ as a favourite of MRAs and red-pillers, I see it as a film advancing feminism too. _Gone Girl_ presents a strong female villain which almost never exists. If actresses need better roles to prove they are as talented as men, better roles need to be written. No matter how you feel about the character, it is clear that _Gone Girl_'s Amy is such a role.

    - generic [ref=e2766]: RIGH1
    - heading "Right of Way" [level=2] [ref=e2768]:
      - link "Right of Way" [ref=e2769] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#RIGH1"
    - generic [ref=e2770]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2771]: 25 Mar 2015
    - generic [ref=e2772]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2773]: cycling, safety, consequentialism
    - generic [ref=e2774]: illus
    - generic [ref=e2775]:
      - img "a cyclist navigating through an intersection in the middle of a city with lots of traffic, telephoto lens photo"
    - generic [ref=e2776]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2777]: I was thinking about victim-blaming and consequentialism as I walked home yesterday and as I crossed a pedestrian crossing, a cyclist was coming quickly down the road. The cyclist stopped to let me cross, and then pedalled hard to regain his lost momentum after I was across. This isn't very sensible. Cyclists have a much harder time decelerating and accelerating than pedestrians and drivers. Pedestrians should give way to cyclists so that they can maintain their momentum and use rolling stops instead of actual stops. It's arguably safer, too. Accidents might happen if a pedestrian assumes cyclists can stop quickly.

    - generic [ref=e2786]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄

    - generic [ref=e2788]: VICT1
    - heading "Victim-blaming and Consequentialism" [level=2] [ref=e2790]:
      - link "Victim-blaming and Consequentialism" [ref=e2791] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#VICT1"
    - generic [ref=e2792]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2793]: 24 Mar 2015
    - generic [ref=e2794]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2795]: ethics, consequentialism, blame
    - generic [ref=e2796]: illus
    - generic [ref=e2797]:
      - img "a sketchy person in a trenchcoat in a dark alley, noir lighting, charcoal sketch"
    - generic [ref=e2798]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2799]: "A student wishes to take a shortcut through a dark alleyway and knows they risk being attacked. They take the shortcut anyway, and get mugged. Deontologists blame the aggressor entirely: the victim had a right to not be harmed. Consequentialists might argue that the student knew the risks and is partially responsible for the consequences of their choice. This is uncomfortable logic. In response we can either (1) reject consequentialism, (2) accept that we should blame victims (at least privately), or (3) refine the consequentialist calculus to stop victim-blaming. Maybe we could argue that optimal consequences require systems of rights?"

    - generic [ref=e2810]: EFFE1
    - heading "Effective Vegan Advocacy" [level=2] [ref=e2812]:
      - link "Effective Vegan Advocacy" [ref=e2813] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#EFFE1"
    - generic [ref=e2814]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2815]: 10 Mar 2015
    - generic [ref=e2816]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2817]: veganism, climate, advocacy
    - generic [ref=e2818]: illus
    - generic [ref=e2819]:
      - img "a happy person making food for friends, health food ad poster"
    - generic [ref=e2820]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2821]: I try to eat lots of vegan food. The livestock industry produces somewhere between 18% and 51% of global greenhouse gas emissions and eating less meat and dairy is the easiest way to reduce the pollution you cause. Pure veganism, though, can be a chore when you are pelted with advertising for beef and milk. Applying the Pareto principle, 80% of the reduction could probably be done for 20% of the effort, and vice versa. Convince others that 80% veganism is easy and more emissions will be reduced than trying and failing to convince people to go straight to 100%.

      - link "Fossil Fuel Divestment" [ref=e2888] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#FOSS1"
    - generic [ref=e2889]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2890]: 27 Sept 2014
    - generic [ref=e2891]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2892]: climate, finance, activism
    - generic [ref=e2893]: illus
    - generic [ref=e2894]:
      - img "a protest at wall street with lots of bankers holding picket signs decorated with dollar signs, telephoto lens"
    - generic [ref=e2895]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2896]: "I'm not convinced that divestment from fossil fuel companies is effective. I think the financial arguments being put forth are very convincing: fossil fuel companies likely have a lot of reserves which are going to be stranded as people take climate change more seriously and investment funds which have a long-term focus should be calculating this into their investment strategies. But if the purpose is to starve fossil fuel companies of investment, can it ever succeed? Part of my scepticism comes from a worry that other investors will happily buy the divested shares because their price-to-earnings ratio is slightly lower."

    - generic [ref=e2909]: INCL1
    - heading "Inclusive Feminist Labels" [level=2] [ref=e2911]:
      - link "Inclusive Feminist Labels" [ref=e2912] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#INCL1"
    - generic [ref=e2913]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2914]: 4 May 2014
    - generic [ref=e2915]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2916]: feminism, language, advocacy
    - generic [ref=e2917]: illus
    - generic [ref=e2918]:
      - img "men standing up for female colleagues in a meeting, stock photo, colored pencil drawing"
    - generic [ref=e2919]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2920]: Adam Grant wrote about the scepticism that men face when exploring feminism. ("Was he trying to ingratiate himself with women to improve his dating prospects?") I worry about this myself. What I found most interesting in what he wrote, though, was the effect of inclusive group names and labels. Labelling an organisation "Princeton Men and Women Opposed to Proposition 174" encouraged more advocacy for other genders than "Princeton Opponents of Proposition 174". _Feminism_ is a label used to describe advocacy for women's rights. But it also extends to the promotion of gender equality for everyone. Is the label "feminist" exclusive?

    - generic [ref=e2933]: THEG1
    - heading "The Great Depression" [level=2] [ref=e2935]:
      - link "The Great Depression" [ref=e2936] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#THEG1"
    - generic [ref=e2937]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2938]: 23 Apr 2014
    - generic [ref=e2939]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2940]: history, inequality, resilience
    - generic [ref=e2941]: illus
    - generic [ref=e2942]:
      - img "historic sepia photo of a wagon in the great depression going down a dusty road with rows of orange trees"
    - generic [ref=e2943]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2944]: I was sitting by a pool in a sunny resort in Fiji. Next to me there was a piña colada with condensation beading on the glass. And I couldn't put down _The Grapes of Wrath_ by John Steinbeck. The pain and poverty of its heroes was jarring against the luxurious, tropical backdrop. After adjusting for inflation, someone in 1937 would need to have picked over half a US ton of peaches to pay for the piña colada I had. Nonetheless, the novel has unrelenting optimism about perserverence through hardship. Meanwhile, Benjamin Graham made 14.7% per annum during the Great Depression.

    - generic [ref=e2959]: TRUS1
    - heading "Trust in Expertise" [level=2] [ref=e2961]:
      - link "Trust in Expertise" [ref=e2962] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#TRUS1"
    - generic [ref=e2963]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2964]: 8 Apr 2014
    - generic [ref=e2965]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2966]: epistemology, science, authority
    - generic [ref=e2967]: illus
    - generic [ref=e2968]:
      - img "a person standing in front of a globe, looking thoughtful"
    - generic [ref=e2969]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2970]: We are all jacks of many trades and experts of only a few, if any. Despite this, opinions about everything are easy to develop and even easier to publicise. We encounter difficult questions about subjects unknown to us, such as climate science. In these situations, it is unreasonable to expect us to find our own answers. Instead, we rely on experts and we trust their judgment about their topics of study. Suppose that you cannot trust experts; they might be colluding to mislead you. Can you trust yourself to understand the complex topics more than you can trust the experts?

    - generic [ref=e2983]: WOME1
    - heading "Women Aren't Crazy" [level=2] [ref=e2985]:
      - link "Women Aren't Crazy" [ref=e2986] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#WOME1"
    - generic [ref=e2987]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e2988]: 24 Mar 2014
    - generic [ref=e2989]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e2990]: sexism, stereotypes, feminism
    - generic [ref=e2991]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e2992]: Yashar Ali wrote a good summary of how dismissing women's negative reactions to sexism can make women feel like they are being irrational. There is an unfair stereotype that women are crazy, so if a woman responds to sexism in a negative way, she must be 'overreacting'. Saying "you're so sensitive!" undermines her perspective. Some people see this rejection of women's opinions as inconsiderate, most don't notice it at all. But it fuels a cruel stereotype by muting women's viewpoints, as it seeds self-doubt and self-censoring. If this causes women to be hesitant to speak up, communities lose valuable voices.
  - article [ref=e2993]:
    - generic [ref=e2994]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄

    - heading "Sorted by Colour" [level=2] [ref=e2998]:
      - link "Sorted by Colour" [ref=e2999] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#SORT1"
    - generic [ref=e3000]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e3001]: 18 Mar 2014
    - generic [ref=e3002]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e3003]: books, aesthetics, algorithms
    - generic [ref=e3004]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e3005]: I was reading the newly-launched FiveThirtyEight blog today (it's not difficult to notice my nerd crush on Nate Silver) and he used his bookcase as an example of the 'trade-off between vividness and scalability'. My wife and I have been sorting our books by spine colour for a while now, but it's not as impressive as his. I have been curious about 'objective' algorithms for sorting by spine colour because some multi-colour books, like Nassim Nicholas Taleb's Antifragile, just refuse to be cleanly categorised. What about fitting spine rectangles into a 2-dimensional bookcase rectangle, optimising for minimal distance between similarly coloured pixels?

    - generic [ref=e3013]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e3014]: 6 Jan 2014
    - generic [ref=e3015]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e3016]: gaming, storytelling, burnout
    - generic [ref=e3017]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e3018]: I met my wife playing Dungeons & Dragons. The world-building, improv, and camaraderie made it feel like the perfect hobby for me, but a few factors have caused me to drop it. I'm geographically further away from my D&D group than I was during university (albeit most players still live in the same city as me) and I don't have as much free time to waste on map-making, which was one of the best parts. Finally, the stress from juggling increasingly powerful players alongside expanding permutations of game-breaking abilities burned me out. Even so, I'm feeling drawn to DMing again.

I rolled my eyes and thought about how overrated they were, but then felt bad. I don't wear them myself, but I think I might be the kind of person to post photos of how they looked if I did. I don't particularly care about leggings, but I just thought it was interesting how I reacted negatively to a stream of photos.

    - generic [ref=e3035]: OFRA1
    - heading "O Frabjous Day!" [level=2] [ref=e3037]:
      - link "O Frabjous Day!" [ref=e3038] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#OFRA1"
    - generic [ref=e3039]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e3040]: 1 May 2013
    - generic [ref=e3041]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e3042]: poetry, language, romance
    - generic [ref=e3043]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e3044]: One of the sexiest things I've ever seen was one of my best friends impressing a girl by reciting Lewis Caroll's 'Jabberwocky' to her from memory. The poem shows why Alice's pair of books are incredibly insightful into the puzzles of language. Half of the words (like 'vorpal' and 'chortle') are made up, but the words still have meanings (even if Alice doesn't 'exactly know what they are'). We derive meanings from context, allowing us to learn new vocabulary. Even when half of the words are made up, we can understand them from how they reflect off of known words.
  - article [ref=e3045]:
    - generic [ref=e3046]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄

    - generic [ref=e3048]: REGU1
    - heading "Regulating Hate Speech" [level=2] [ref=e3050]:
      - link "Regulating Hate Speech" [ref=e3051] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#REGU1"
    - generic [ref=e3052]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e3053]: 30 Apr 2013
    - generic [ref=e3054]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e3055]: free-speech, ethics, harm
    - generic [ref=e3056]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e3057]: Where does the line get drawn about what can be said? Dissent? Ignorant hatred? Inciting imminent violence? Trolling religious zealots? I generally believe that the better response to hate speech is more speech, presented in a civil manner, to expose the ignorance of the haters. But how does that protect the victims of hate speech? When racial slurs are aimed at children, how should a liberal respond? A hedonistic liberal would say that liberty is virtuous only for the sake of making people happier. Freedom of speech almost always does that, but I can imagine situations when it does not.

    - generic [ref=e3069]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e3070]: As I hope you have noticed, I put at least as much effort into visually laying out my blog posts as I do into writing them. I carefully use Markdown to format my text and GIMP to shoop my decorative images maybe obsessively. So, when the Tumblr app on my phone strips my drafts of their formatting, it frustrates me. Back when I was a teenager, I would evangelise OpenOffice.org as a reasonable alternative to Microsoft Office, but it was difficult to justify making my documents look nice if exporting to Word was just going to butcher the layout.

    - heading "_Sucker Punch_ Thoughts" [level=2] [ref=e3076]:
      - link "_Sucker Punch_ Thoughts" [ref=e3077] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#SUCK1"
    - generic [ref=e3078]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e3079]: 29 Apr 2013
    - generic [ref=e3080]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e3081]: film, feminism, critique
    - generic [ref=e3082]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e3083]: "_Sucker Punch_ suffers from problems like poor pacing and shallow character depth, but while the form has obscured the message, the message is much more interesting than the story or visual effects. On one side of the coin, you are presented with a criticism of modern female exploitation: these animated exaggerations are not real women -- they are geek fantasies. But on the other side, the film is shamelessly indulging in the very fetishes it criticises. The sucker punch is thrown at the audience, drawing them in to see a video game fantasy, and then reprimanding them by breaking their toys."

    - paragraph [ref=e3096]: A month ago we put an avocado stone and some spring onion stumps in water and placed them on the window sill.   - article [ref=e3097]:
    - generic [ref=e3098]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e3099]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e3100]: DRIN1
    - heading "Drinking and Driving" [level=2] [ref=e3102]:
      - link "Drinking and Driving" [ref=e3103] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#DRIN1"
    - generic [ref=e3104]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e3105]: 26 Apr 2013
    - generic [ref=e3106]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e3107]: ethics, risk, law
    - generic [ref=e3108]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e3109]: When someone is criminally punished only for driving while intoxicated, they are not being punished for an action which caused harm but because they performed a combination of actions which are statistically significantly linked with harm being caused. Due to an element of moral "luck", it is certainly possible for a drunk or high driver to drive somewhere without causing harm but we still consider that criminal. How does the act consequentialist reconcile this? Argue that driving drunk is not intrinsically wrong? Or develop a possibly convoluted argument for how each individual action of driving drunk somehow causes harm indirectly?
  - article [ref=e3110]:
    - generic [ref=e3111]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e3112]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e3113]: CODE1
    - heading "Code as Art?" [level=2] [ref=e3115]:
      - link "Code as Art?" [ref=e3116] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#CODE1"
    - generic [ref=e3117]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e3118]: 25 Apr 2013
    - generic [ref=e3119]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e3120]: software, aesthetics, art
    - generic [ref=e3121]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e3122]: "I was arguing/discussing with my future wife about what is considered \"art\". I believe that the artist's intentions are irrelevant and that whether something is \"art\" is determined by how the viewer observes it. Looking for counterexamples and illustrations, I asked her: would you consider it possible for a computer program to be \"art\"? I would, but she wasn't sure. Undoubtedly, almost all computer programs are primarily functional, but there is elegant code and there is inelegant code. Does elegance make code artistic? If it does, I should start a gallery of clips of elegant code from open-source software."

    - paragraph [ref=e3133]: sacrifice, commemoration, duty
    - generic [ref=e3134]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e3135]: I attended a dawn memorial service for ANZAC veterans this morning and thought about how annual commemoration for military personnel serves as a type of intangible compensation for their unusually sacrificial employment. A soldier going to war risks much more than I do when I go to work, but I do not believe they get a "heroism" bonus in their paycheck. Instead, our social contract teaches us to "pay" them our respect. A similar honour is bestowed upon public servants with life-threatening careers such as firefighting and law enforcement. Do we have a duty to remember and enshrine our valiant?

    - generic [ref=e3234]: MULT1
    - heading "Multiculturalism and Competition" [level=2] [ref=e3236]:
      - link "Multiculturalism and Competition" [ref=e3237] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#MULT1"
    - generic [ref=e3238]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e3239]: 28 Nov 2012
    - generic [ref=e3240]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e3241]: economics, multiculturalism, tolerance
    - generic [ref=e3242]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e3243]: My understanding of compassion in free market economics is that reducing the prices of goods and services through increasing market efficiency will help poor _consumers_ more than government intervention will help poor _workers_. But there are barriers to entry which are avoidable and are not caused by government intervention. Does aversion to multiculturalism act as a barrier to entry? When a fat, white woman yells "Speak English!" at a Chinese shopkeeper, the shopkeeper pays a hidden cost to continue working. In a maximally competitive market, where race shouldn't affect labour, neither should foreign cultural expression. More tolerance brings more competition.

    - heading "Policing Protests Better" [level=2] [ref=e3301]:
      - link "Policing Protests Better" [ref=e3302] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#POLI1"
    - generic [ref=e3303]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e3304]: 6 Nov 2012
    - generic [ref=e3305]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e3306]: politics, protests, policing
    - generic [ref=e3307]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e3308]: As I showered this morning, I imagined how I would act as police chief in charge of maintaining law and order during an "Occupy Wall Street"-style protest. Balancing the goals of prosecuting trespassers and keeping the peace is undeniably a difficult task, but I think that "perception" of police brutality against protesters creates a Streisand effect by rallying more support for the protesters and encouraging rioters and violent opportunists. Wouldn't it be better to videotape any non-violent crimes (like trespassing) and not evict protesters, waiting for them leave out of boredom? The police can then prosecute peacefully later.

    - heading "Rhetoric Through Repetition" [level=2] [ref=e3314]:
      - link "Rhetoric Through Repetition" [ref=e3315] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#RHET1"
    - generic [ref=e3316]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e3317]: 6 Sept 2012
    - generic [ref=e3318]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e3319]: rhetoric, language, persuasion
    - generic [ref=e3320]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e3321]: Reading Joseph Romm's Language Intelligence has led me to pick up my previously put-aside interest in practicing effective rhetoric. With my background in philosophy and computer science, I have overvalued the role that unemotional logic has to play in crafting convincing speech. Just as I repeated my lines again and again and again when learning to play Friar Lawrence in school, I have been repeating the lines of Martin Luther King Jr's "I Have a Dream". Starting with _anaphora_ and alliteration, I am keeping a keen eye on figures of speech that focus my future approaches to effective arguing.

    - generic [ref=e3325]: ARCT1
    - heading "Arctic Sea Ice" [level=2] [ref=e3327]:
      - link "Arctic Sea Ice" [ref=e3328] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#ARCT1"
    - generic [ref=e3329]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e3330]: 29 Aug 2012
    - generic [ref=e3331]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e3332]: climate, arctic, sea-ice
    - generic [ref=e3333]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e3334]: This Arctic summer has broken records for the least sea ice in recent history. Lots of that melted ice will return this winter, but the total thick ice is decreasing. There are two issues to note. First, this melting trend exemplifies the clarity of the physical evidence for global warming. While Arctic sea ice levels alone do not establish human attribution, they identify much-needed climatic research. Second, the distinction between single- and multi-year ice highlights feedback loops. As more ice thaws and refreezes, less remains thick enough to maintain the albedo needed to prevent extra absorption of sunlight, accelerating warming.
  - article [ref=e3335]:
    - generic [ref=e3336]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e3337]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e3338]: BLOG1
    - heading "Blogging About Myself" [level=2] [ref=e3340]:
      - link "Blogging About Myself" [ref=e3341] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#BLOG1"
    - generic [ref=e3342]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e3343]: 28 Aug 2012
    - generic [ref=e3344]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e3345]: blogging, identity, self-promotion
    - generic [ref=e3346]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e3347]: Wouldn't only a narcissist create a website about themselves? I benefit and suffer from an inflated ego, of course. I wouldn't consider my opinions worth blogging about if I didn't. But I don't think that my blog was intended as a soapbox for me to grab attention. 21st century identities are forged much more so on the internet than 20th century identities, and with a blog and a Twitter handle and a Facebook wall and a Google+ profile, I can manage the definition of "Matt Heard" more effectively. If I don't, computers will deduce my personality from my search terms.
  - article [ref=e3348]:

    - generic [ref=e3351]: INTR1
    - heading "_Introducing Political Philosophy_" [level=2] [ref=e3353]:
      - link "_Introducing Political Philosophy_" [ref=e3354] [cursor=pointer]:

    - generic [ref=e3355]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e3356]: 24 Aug 2012
    - generic [ref=e3357]: tags
    - paragraph [ref=e3358]: philosophy, politics, reading
    - generic [ref=e3359]: text
    - paragraph [ref=e3360]: "I have a habit of reading in sprints, consuming several books in one month and then not picking up any for the next several months. My future wife and I challenged each other to read more by starting from one end of our bookcase and reading each book one at a time. I just finished my second book: _Introducing Political Philosophy_. I was cheating, though, because it is short and each page has a large picture. But it's interesting and seems to be a good summary of the history of political thought. My next is _Brave New World_ by Aldous Huxley."
  - article [ref=e3361]:
    - generic [ref=e3362]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e3363]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄
    - generic [ref=e3364]: WHYM1
    - heading "Why Must Everybody Like You?" [level=2] [ref=e3366]:
      - link "Why Must Everybody Like You?" [ref=e3367] [cursor=pointer]:
        - /url: "#WHYM1"
    - generic [ref=e3368]: pubAt
    - paragraph [ref=e3369]: 5 May 2006
    - generic [ref=e3370]: illus
    - generic [ref=e3372]: text
    - blockquote [ref=e3373]:
      - paragraph [ref=e3386]: "\"Why must everybody like you? Who liked J. P. Morgan? Was he impressive? In a Turkish bath he'd look like a butcher. But with his pockets on he was very well liked.\" (Miller, 72)"
    - paragraph [ref=e3387]: First of all, I was disappointed by Circa's 3rd of May performance of "Death of a Salesman" - not because the play was so badly done (it was great), but instead because my expectations were so high. For the few days before viewing it, I received the notion that the play in general, and also this Circa presentation of it were brilliant. I heard nothing but criticism for both, and so my expectations ran rampant and free.
    - paragraph [ref=e3388]: I criticise the play in general for trying so hard to be depressing, and although it succeeded at achieving this, it seems like a cheap attraction for audiences. We go to horror films to watch people in situations we wouldn't enjoy being in ourselves - watch _Saw_ or its sequel. (I haven't watched _Saw_ but from the impression I get from others who have watched it, it's addictive in a disturbing way.) I believe that people watch depressing films and plays to cheer themselves up, and I disagree with how a lot of people believe that making a film or play depressing makes it a better film or play.
    - paragraph [ref=e3389]: "The play presents the idea of the \"American Dream\" and how it fails to transfer from an idealistic perspective of the social and economic world to a realistic perspective. The main fault of the idea is that although everyone may be capable of achieving success and reaching the top of the metaphorical mountain, not everyone can be on top, so people must fail for others to succeed. And this is where the majority of criticism of capitalism comes from: for one person to become richer, another must become poorer."
    - paragraph [ref=e3390]: I disagree with the idea that the "American Dream" is a failure, as it is based on ideals which *are* applicable to a realistic perspective of the social and economic world - ideals such as needing to put in hard work to succeed and that if everyone is treated fairly, everyone has the ability to become successful.
    - paragraph [ref=e3391]: "Another aspect of the \"American Dream\" is that America is where dreams come true. Before dismissing this as illogical because geography cannot determine 'luck' or 'ability', the statistics need to be looked at:"
    - paragraph [ref=e3392]: In 2004, the US had an average income of $41400US ($64703NZ) while New Zealand had an average income of $20310US ($31747NZ). That's right, the average income of the US is double of that of New Zealand.
    - paragraph [ref=e3393]: I'm not trying to show off or anything; my point is that the US is a land of opportunity because there is a different atmosphere of competition and hard work.
    - paragraph [ref=e3394]: In California, school was all about competition. You had to get better than a B (85%) on every test to stay in the top class, and that isn't easy. I had a friend who was scared to tell his parents that he got less than 90% on a Biology exam.
    - paragraph [ref=e3395]: I must admit that it's to the other extreme, but the lack of competitive attitude and the idea of "she'll be right..." has caused New Zealand to fail to achieve its potential. Of course, I'm speaking in generalisations. There is a group of extraordinary New Zealanders who manage to break through this lacking atmosphere to reach their full potential. And I hope to be one of them, one day.
  - article [ref=e3396]:
    - generic [ref=e3397]: ▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄▄

    - heading "_Scary Movie IV_ / _Closer_" [level=2] [ref=e3401]:
